10129{_}1616{_}For a _holy_ night.
10129{_}1617{_}Confession.
10129{_}1618{_}For every one for whom I have been specially asked to pray.
10129{_}1619{_}_SPECIAL SUBJECTS_.
10129{_}1620{_}SUNDAY.
10129{_}1621{_}That I may make the most of Sabbath hours and gain much from the Word.
10129{_}1622{_}Deliverance from wandering thoughts.
10129{_}1623{_}_Pure_ praise.
10129{_}1624{_}Blessing on services and choir.
10129{_}1625{_}_Hallowed be Thy name_.
10129{_}1626{_}Intercessions.
10129{_}1627{_}(Initials of many clergymen, of her brother, her god-children, and "our servants.")
10129{_}1628{_}MONDAY--"For joy and peace."
10129{_}1629{_}That the life of Jesus may be manifest in me.
10129{_}1630{_}_Thy kingdom come_  Intercession for Church Missionary Society and Irish Society.
10129{_}1631{_}(Initials of her eldest sister, _all_ her family, and "Oakhampton servants.")
10129{_}1632{_}TUESDAY--"For longsuffering"  That my unconscious influence may be all for Him.
10129{_}1633{_}_Thy will be done_.
10129{_}1634{_}Intercession for Mildmay (and initials of her brother Henry's children and many Leamington friends).
10129{_}1635{_}'WEDNESDAY--"Gentleness."
10129{_}1636{_}For spirit of prayer and shadowless communion.
10129{_}1637{_}_Give us this day our daily bread._  Intercession for the universities and public schools, for many friends, for M.V.G.H., and E.C.
10129{_}1638{_}THURSDAY--"Goodness."
10129{_}1639{_}For much fruit to His praise.
10129{_}1640{_}Soul-winning.
10129{_}1641{_}Spirit of praise.
10129{_}1642{_}_Forgive us our trespasses_.
10129{_}1643{_}Local work.
10129{_}1644{_}Swansea and Mrs. Morgan.
10129{_}1645{_}For my sister Ellen, all at Winterdyne, "and the servants."
10129{_}1646{_}FRIDAY--"Faith."
10129{_}1647{_}Wisdom to be shown more of His will and commands.
10129{_}1648{_}_Lead us not into temptation_.
10129{_}1649{_}For my brother and all at Upton Bishop.
10129{_}1650{_}SATURDAY--"Meekness and Temperance."
10129{_}1651{_}That the Word of Christ may dwell in me richly, open treasure of Thy Word to me, fill my seed-basket.
10129{_}1652{_}_Deliver us from evil._.
10129{_}1653{_}For the Church of England and the Queen.
10129{_}1654{_}(Initials of many friends.)
10129{_}1655{_}A plan of work for 1879--"If the Lord will"--was sketched out, but it was not the Lord's will that it should he accomplished, and many subjects were not even attempted.
10129{_}1656{_}On her return from London in the early part of the year, her friends noticed the peculiar gladness of her service.
10129{_}1657{_}She said one morning to her sister, "Marie, it is really very remarkable how everything I do seems to prosper and nourish.
10129{_}1658{_}There is my 'Bruey Branch' growing and increasing, and now the temperance work, and so many letters tell me that God is blessing my little books."
10129{_}1659{_}The "Bruey Branch" here mentioned was an effort to get children to interest themselves in the Irish Society, and met with signal success.
10129{_}1660{_}It had been started two years previously with eight collectors--now hundreds of collecting cards had gone out.
10129{_}1661{_}Of her temperance work she writes a little later: "May, 1879.
10129{_}1662{_}I haven't taken up teetotal work, but teetotal work has taken up me!
10129{_}1663{_}Morgan and Scott made me accept a big handsome pledge-book in February, and somehow the thing has fairly _caught fire_ here.
10129{_}1664{_}One led to another, and yesterday boys were coming all day to sign.
10129{_}1665{_}I had twenty-five recruits yesterday alone, and a whole squad more are coming this evening!
10129{_}1666{_}and we are going in for getting EVERY boy in the whole village!
10129{_}1667{_}and now 'Please, miss, mayn't girls sign?'
10129{_}1668{_}So I've got to open a girls' branch as well!
10129{_}1669{_}So work grows!"
10129{_}1670{_}Again, "Really a wonderful little temperance work here; all the rising generation have joined the pledge except about twelve, and now the men want to speak to me and I am to meet them to-night at the corner of the village (open air, having no place else) with my pledge-book.
10129{_}1671{_}I have got 118 pledged, and each with prayer over it and personal talk about better things."
10129{_}1672{_}On May 21 she met these men, carrying with her her Bible and temperance book.
10129{_}1673{_}While standing, heavy clouds came up, and she was obliged to return home, wet and chilly, though some men were still waiting to speak to her.
10129{_}1674{_}The next day (Thursday) she managed to get to church and received the Lord's Supper.
10129{_}1675{_}She was very tired with the service, and rode home on a donkey.
10129{_}1676{_}As she passed through the village, quite a procession of her boys followed her.
10129{_}1677{_}She urged her donkey boy to "leave the devil's side and get on the safe side; that Jesus Christ was the winning side; that He loved him, and was calling him, and wouldn't he choose Him for his Captain?"
10129{_}1678{_}Arrived at home, she ran in for her temperance book, and the boy signed it on the saddle.
10129{_}1679{_}That evening she spoke to several persons with intense earnestness and pleading.
10129{_}1680{_}The next day she was to have attended a temperance meeting and have presented 150 cards to those who had signed her book; but the chilliness increased, and the doctor forbade her to go out.
10129{_}1681{_}Unable to be present herself, she sent two messages by her sister: one to those who had signed--"Behold, God Himself is ... our Captain;" [1] to those who had not signed--"Come thou with us and we will do thee good."
10129{_}1682{_}[2]--While the meeting was going on she was busy at home stitching strong paper tract-bags for sailors at sea, till she felt ill and had to be assisted to her room.
10129{_}1683{_}[Footnote 1: 2 Chron.
10129{_}1684{_}xiii.
10129{_}1685{_}12.]
10129{_}1686{_}[Footnote 2: Num.
10129{_}1687{_}x.
10129{_}1688{_}29.]
10129{_}1689{_}On May 26 she was able to correct the proof of _Morning Stars_, on the text, "I am the bright and morning Star;" and then, as her sister says, the pen so long used in the service of her King was laid down.
10129{_}1690{_}The last passage she looked at in her Bible was the _Christian Progress_ chapter for May 28.
10129{_}1691{_}[1] She asked that it might be read to her, and dwelt on "Be thou faithful unto death, and I will give thee a crown of life."
10129{_}1692{_}On the 29th fever and internal inflammation rapidly came on, and she exhibited all the symptoms of peritonitis.
10129{_}1693{_}She suffered very severe pain; but though the outward man was perishing, the inward man was being renewed.
10129{_}1694{_}On May 30, speaking of justification by faith, she said, "Not for our own works or deservings; oh, what vanity it seems now to rest on our own obedience for salvation, any merit of our own takes away the glory of the atoning blood.
10129{_}1695{_}'Unto Him that loved us, and washed us from our sins in His own blood'--_that's it_."
10129{_}1696{_}When asked if she had any fear, she replied, "Why should I?
10129{_}1697{_}Jesus said, 'It is finished;' and what was His precious blood shed for?
10129{_}1698{_}_I trust that_."
10129{_}1699{_}[Footnote 1: Rev.
10129{_}1700{_}ii.
10129{_}1701{_}1-10.]
10129{_}1702{_}On Whit-Sunday she was better, and able to converse a little with her brother and sister.
10129{_}1703{_}On the following day at early dawn she made the remark, "'Spite of the breakers, Marie, I am so happy.
10129{_}1704{_}God's promises are so true.
10129{_}1705{_}Not a fear."
10129{_}1706{_}At 8 A.M. it was thought she was departing.
10129{_}1707{_}The Lord's Supper was administered at her request, and when it was over she whispered to her brother, "Frank dear, it is not the performance of the rite, _no safety in that_; but it is obedience to His command, and as a _remembrance_ of His dying love."
10129{_}1708{_}When the doctor told her she would soon be going home, she exclaimed.
10129{_}1709{_}"Beautiful!
10129{_}1710{_}too good to be true!...
10129{_}1711{_}Oh!
10129{_}1712{_}it is the Lord Jesus that is so dear to me, I can't tell how precious!
10129{_}1713{_}how much He has been to me!"
10129{_}1714{_}Afterwards she asked for "How sweet the name of Jesus sounds!"
10129{_}1715{_}To the vicar of Swansea, who visited her, she said, "Oh!
10129{_}1716{_}I want all of you to speak _bright, bright_ words about Jesus, oh, do, _do_!
10129{_}1717{_}It is all perfect peace.
10129{_}1718{_}I am only waiting for Jesus to take me in."
10129{_}1719{_}Her sufferings were very acute, and when told how patient she had been that even the doctors noticed it, she replied, "Oh!
10129{_}1720{_}I am so glad you tell me this.
10129{_}1721{_}I did want to glorify Him every step of the way, and especially in this suffering.
10129{_}1722{_}I hope none of you will have five minutes of this pain."
10129{_}1723{_}On Tuesday, June 3, she was evidently worse.
10129{_}1724{_}Among the words she uttered were these: "I am lost in amazement!
10129{_}1725{_}There hath not failed one word of all His promise!"
10129{_}1726{_}Mentioning the names of many dear ones, she said, "I want _all_ to come to me in heaven; oh!
10129{_}1727{_}don't, _don't_ disappoint me; tell them, 'Trust Jesus.'"
10129{_}1728{_}When one of her sisters repeated the words of the hymn, "Jesus, I will trust Thee," she sang the verse right through to her tune "Hermas."
10129{_}1729{_}Violent sickness ensued, and when it was over she folded her hands on her heart, saying, "There, now it is all over!
10129{_}1730{_}Blessed rest!"
10129{_}1731{_}Her sister thus describes the glorious sunset of her life on June 3, 1879, at the age of 42: "And now she looked up steadfastly as if she saw the Lord; and surely nothing less heavenly could have reflected such a glorious radiance upon her face.
10129{_}1732{_}For ten minutes we watched that almost visible meeting with her King, and her countenance was so glad, as if she were already talking to Him.
10129{_}1733{_}Then she tried to sing, but after one sweet high note--'HE--,' her voice failed, and as her brother commended her soul into her Redeemer's hand, she passed away.
10129{_}1734{_}Our precious sister was gone, satisfied, glorified, within the palace of her King."
10129{_}1735{_}And so she fell asleep, and her eyes saw the King in His beauty--that King of whom she sung so sweetly and wrote so loyally.
10129{_}1736{_}On June 9 they laid her body to rest in the quiet churchyard of Astley Church in Worcestershire.
10129{_}1737{_}And thus within sight of the room which saw her birth, her body lies "until the day dawn."
10129{_}1738{_}VIII.
10129{_}1739{_}"UNDER THE SURFACE."
10129{_}1740{_}_Upon_ the surface you saw a bright, accomplished lady.
10129{_}1741{_}She had marked ability as a linguist.
10129{_}1742{_}She acquired a great deal of German as a child by carefully attending while present at the German lessons given to her sisters.
10129{_}1743{_}She learnt enough Greek and Hebrew to read her Hebrew Bible and to enjoy her Greek Testament, and often brings out in her letters the fact that she had been studying it.
10129{_}1744{_}As we have seen, she was an accomplished musician, and she was untiring in her literary productions.
10129{_}1745{_}Her books of poems comprise _Life-Chords,_ consisting of "Under His shadow,"--"Her last poems"--"Loyal Responses," and "Her earlier poems;" _Life Mosaic_, comprising "The Ministry of Song," and "Under the Surface;" _Swiss Letters and Alpine Poems_, written during several tours in Switzerland.
10129{_}1746{_}Her chief prose works _Kept for the Master's Use, The Royal Invitation, My King, Royal Commandments, Royal Bounty, Starlight through Shadows, Morning Stars, Morning Bells, Little Pillows, and Bruey, a little Worker for Christ_.
10129{_}1747{_}_Upon_ the surface, too, you saw a woman of sound-common sense.
10129{_}1748{_}This was evidenced both in her writings, and her daily life.
10129{_}1749{_}For example, she writes thus one day: "I felt as if I rather wanted a little intellectual bracing, as if something of contact with intellect were necessary to prevent my getting into a weak and wishy-washy kind of thought and language.
10129{_}1750{_}I like intellects to rub against and have no present access to books which would do it, so I bethought myself of seeing what Shakespeare would do for me and I think my motive was really that I might polish my own instruments for the Master's use."
10129{_}1751{_}Again, as regards dress her sensible comment was, "If the King's daughter is to be 'all glorious within,' she must not be outwardly a fright!
10129{_}1752{_}I must dress both as a lady and a Christian.
10129{_}1753{_}The question of cost I see very strongly, and do not consider myself at liberty to spend on dress that which might be spared for God's work; but it costs no more to have a thing well and prettily made."
10129{_}1754{_}Yes, _on_ the surface you saw an accomplished lady, and on the surface you saw also beaming out the fact that _under_ the surface she was a whole-hearted Christian.
10129{_}1755{_}This was the most marked feature in her character.
10129{_}1756{_}No one could be in her company five minutes without recognising Whose she was and Whom she served.
10129{_}1757{_}A clergyman, who knew perhaps more of her inner life than any one else, in a letter to the writer, says, "The two most prominent characteristics of the last five and a half years of her life seemed to me to be her unreserved consecration and her absolute confidence in the Lord and His Word."
10129{_}1758{_}The preceding chapters will have shown the reader how true an estimate this is.
10129{_}1759{_}The business of her life was to glorify God and enjoy Him for ever.
10129{_}1760{_}Of delicate health, she might have spent a large portion of her time in fretful complainings; but she looked to her Heavenly Father to consecrate even her sicknesses to His service.
10129{_}1761{_}Her standard of Christian life was a very high one.
10129{_}1762{_}She thus writes of a friend: "I write to you as one who is really wanting to follow Jesus altogether, really wanting to live and speak _exactly_ according to His commands and His beautiful example; and where this is the standard, what seems a little thing or nothing at all to others, is sure to be sin, because it is disobeying His dear Word and not 'following, _fully_.'"
10129{_}1763{_}Her intimate knowledge of Scripture, her sound common-sense kept her from falling into many of the errors into which some who have aimed high in holy things have fallen.
10129{_}1764{_}In a letter to her sister on this subject, she thus expresses herself: "As to 'perfectionism' or 'sinlessness,' I have all along, and over and over again, said I never did and do not hold either.
10129{_}1765{_}'Sinlessness' belongs _only_ to Christ now, and to our glorified state in heaven.
10129{_}1766{_}I believe it to be not merely an impossibility on earth but an actual contradiction of our very being, which cannot be 'sinless' till the resurrection change has passed upon us.
10129{_}1767{_}But being kept from falling, kept from sins, is quite another thing, and the Bible seems to teem with commands and promises about it.
10129{_}1768{_}First, however, I would distinctly state, that it is _only_ as and while a soul is under the full power of the blood of Christ that it can be cleansed from all sin; that one moment's withdrawal from that power, and it is again actively, because really, sinning;... one instant of standing alone is certain fall."
10129{_}1769{_}While magnifying the Saviour's power to save, she had a just estimate of her own condition; only about two years before her death she thus expresses herself: "I can say for myself that I feel I have deserved the very suffering of hell for my transgression of the first great commandment of the law ('Thou shalt love the Lord thy God,' etc.
10129{_}1770{_}), and for my sin of unbelief."
10129{_}1771{_}While she aimed high, she knew full well that she had not attained, neither was already perfect.
10129{_}1772{_}"As to sanctification, that it is the work of the Holy Spirit, and progressive, is the very thing I see and rejoice in.
10129{_}1773{_}He has brought me into the 'highway of holiness' up which I trust every day to progress, continually pressing forward, led by the Spirit of God."
10129{_}1774{_}The simplicity of her trust in God and His Word comes out strikingly in her writings.
10129{_}1775{_}She seems to have grasped the fact that Jesus Christ was "a living bright reality," pledged to uphold and help and comfort all who go to Him as little children.
10129{_}1776{_}Another marked feature in her life was her deep sympathy with others in their trouble and anxieties.
10129{_}1777{_}And this spirit of unselfishness enabled her in her prose writings and her hymns to inspire something of her simple trust into those who read them with receptive minds.
10129{_}1778{_}To see under the surface of Frances Ridley Havergal's character, look into her works, and you find the humble servant of Jesus Christ revealed.
10129{_}1779{_}She "walked with God," and by the attraction of a life bright with the beauty of holiness revealing itself in her writings, she has exercised and still exercises a great power upon Christians by lifting them up to a higher walk with God.
10129{_}1780{_}And many singers will doubtless join hereafter in the song of "Moses and the Lamb" whose souls were on earth attuned to heavenly music through the pleading words or holy example of Frances Ridley Havergal.
10129{_}1781{_}John P. Hobson, M.A.
10129{_}1782{_}HANNAH MORE.
10129{_}1783{_}I.
10129{_}1784{_}EARLY II  Amongst the staunchest supporters of Presbyterianism in the days of Charles II.
10129{_}1785{_}were the Mores of Harleston, Norfolk.
10129{_}1786{_}Glorying in the risk incurred of proscription and imprisonment, they turned their dwelling into a conventicle.
10129{_}1787{_}Here the faithful gathered stealthily at midnight to hear the Gospel preached, whilst one of the house, with drawn sword, stood at the threshold prepared to defend with his life both minister and congregation.
10129{_}1788{_}From this sturdy stock sprang Jacob, the father of Hannah More.
10129{_}1789{_}He married a sensible, high-principled farmer's daughter.
10129{_}1790{_}A family of five girls was born to them, the fourth being Hannah, whose birth occurred on the 2nd of February, 1745.
10129{_}1791{_}Hannah displayed remarkable precocity.
10129{_}1792{_}Before she was four she could repeat the Catechism, much to the astonishment of the parish minister; whilst startling questions about matters far beyond her age were put to those around her.
10129{_}1793{_}At eight her thirst for knowledge increased.
10129{_}1794{_}Sitting on her father's knee she listened eagerly to his recital of the brave deeds of Greeks and Romans and the wise sayings of Plutarch.
10129{_}1795{_}Sometimes her father repeated orations of classic heroes, first in the original tongue, and then in English.
10129{_}1796{_}The interest thus excited led the child to crave for a knowledge of Latin.
10129{_}1797{_}Her father, although averse to girls exceeding the limits of the three "R's" and a few accomplishments, yielded at length to his promising daughter's desire.
10129{_}1798{_}This early introduction to the classics paved the way to a diligent study of Latin in later years and of the best Latin models, which greatly helped in the formation of her literary style.
10129{_}1799{_}She also gained a little knowledge of mathematics; but Euclid had to retire in favour of the less intricate study of French.
10129{_}1800{_}The proficiency which she afterwards acquired in this language she owed to the assiduous tuition of her eldest sister, Mary.
10129{_}1801{_}Before the age of twelve she began to scribble short essays and poems.
10129{_}1802{_}Her systematic education commenced on her becoming a pupil of her sisters' boarding-school at Bristol.
10129{_}1803{_}Here she made rapid progress, often giving convincing proof of intellectual gifts, and before long becoming qualified to assist in tuition.
10129{_}1804{_}In her sixteenth year she was one of Sheridan's most delighted auditors during his delivery of a course of lectures on Eloquence.
10129{_}1805{_}She expressed her admiration in a chaplet of verses which, finding their way into the orator's hands, so impressed him with the fair promise they contained, that he secured an introduction to the author.
10129{_}1806{_}Thus originated one of Hannah's numerous warm friendships of after life.
10129{_}1807{_}Ferguson, the astronomer, was another of Hannah's early acquaintances.
10129{_}1808{_}From him she gained a knowledge of science; whilst he, prompted by his high estimate of her abilities, took counsel with her respecting the style of his literary productions.
10129{_}1809{_}Her intellectual tastes were encouraged and directed, to a large extent, by a somewhat notable Bristol man, of the name of Peach.
10129{_}1810{_}Although a draper by trade, his cultivated mind and excellent literary judgment were of distinct service to his young friend.
10129{_}1811{_}He was entrusted by Hume with the revision of the proof-sheets of the famous History of England.
10129{_}1812{_}A humorous story is related of the interest which Hannah's conversation created in the minds of her elders.
10129{_}1813{_}When laid aside by illness she was attended by a noted physician, Dr. Woodward, who one day became so absorbed in his patient's intellectual discourse that he forgot to make the usual inquiries about her health.
10129{_}1814{_}"Bless me!"
10129{_}1815{_}he exclaimed, as he went downstairs, "I forgot to ask the girl how she was!"
10129{_}1816{_}He returned to the bedside, and rather awkwardly put the formal question to the amused invalid, "How are you to-day, my poor child?"
10129{_}1817{_}Hannah's training in the highest principles of morality and in religion, begun by her devoted parents, received the careful attention of her eldest sister as long as she remained under her care; when out of her teens, she commenced the study of theology under the guidance of Dr. Stonhouse, a clergyman of Bristol.
10129{_}1818{_}At the age of seventeen, finding that the young people in her circle were in the habit of learning passages from plays which frequently savoured of unhealthy sentiment, she conceived the idea of providing a harmless substitute, and thereupon wrote a pastoral drama, _The Search after Happiness_.
10129{_}1819{_}A little later she produced another drama, _The Inflexible Captive_, founded on Metastasio's opera of _Regulus_.
10129{_}1820{_}Encouraged in various ways by numerous friends, on whose judgment she could safely rely, she appears to have taken pains to qualify herself for a literary career.
10129{_}1821{_}She studied Latin, Italian, and Spanish, translated from the best compositions, wrote pieces in imitation of celebrated authors, and thus tried to cultivate her mind, and to form the groundwork of a good and pleasing style.
10129{_}1822{_}Such literary prospects, however, seemed likely to be exchanged for those of a rural domestic life; for at the age of twenty-two she received and accepted an offer of marriage from a country gentleman of wealth and high character.
10129{_}1823{_}The wedding-day was fixed, but was postponed more than once, owing to the bridegroom's indecision.
10129{_}1824{_}At length he lost his chance; for the bride, yielding to the advice of friends, declined to be trifled with any longer, and broke off the engagement.
10129{_}1825{_}To make some amends for his treatment, and to compensate for her resignation, at the prospect of marriage, of her interest in the school which she and her sister were conducting at Bristol, he settled upon her an annuity, and at his death bequeathed her a thousand pounds.
10129{_}1826{_}The settlement was made without her knowledge; and it was not without the utmost difficulty that her friends prevailed in persuading her to agree to the arrangement.
10129{_}1827{_}From this time forward she seems to have set her face against matrimony, for she firmly declined other offers.
10129{_}1828{_}A few years afterwards, on arriving at the age of twenty-eight, a long-cherished wish was realised.
10129{_}1829{_}Since childhood she had longed to visit London.
10129{_}1830{_}As a child her favourite amusement was to make a carriage of a chair, and invite her sisters to ride with her to London "to see bishops and booksellers."
10129{_}1831{_}Through girlhood to womanhood the desire gathered strength.
10129{_}1832{_}In 1773 she set off with two of her sisters to pay her first visit to the Metropolis.
10129{_}1833{_}II.
10129{_}1834{_}IN "VANITY FAIR."
10129{_}1835{_}In order to estimate the complex influences surrounding Hannah More in London, and to appreciate the manner in which she stood the ordeal of passing through "Vanity Fair," it is necessary to bear in mind the social, moral, and religious aspects of the people about the middle of the eighteenth century.
10129{_}1836{_}What are now considered flagrant vices were either unnoticed or tacitly sanctioned.
10129{_}1837{_}Of social refinement, as we now understand the term, there was comparatively little.
10129{_}1838{_}Coarse jokes, swearing, and profanity were almost as common in "polite society" as in the back streets now.
10129{_}1839{_}The literature of the day, excepting the writings of Addison, Johnson, Steele, and a few others, ministered to the low tastes prevalent amongst both the upper and the lower classes.
10129{_}1840{_}Religion had well nigh lost all vitality.
10129{_}1841{_}With the majority of people it had become the subject either of jest, sceptical hostility, or the utmost indifference.
10129{_}1842{_}One of Archbishop Seeker's charges contained the following startling statement:--"In this we cannot be mistaken, that an open and professed disregard of religion is become, through a variety of unhappy causes, the distinguishing character of the present age....
10129{_}1843{_}Indeed, it hath already brought in such dissoluteness and contempt of principle in the higher part of the world, and such profligate intemperance and fearlessness of committing crimes in the lower part, as must, if this torrent of impiety stop not, become absolutely fatal.... Christianity is now ridiculed and railed at with very little reserve; and the teachers of it without any at all."
10129{_}1844{_}[1]  [Footnote 1: Charge to clergy, 1738.
10129{_}1845{_}See vol.
10129{_}1846{_}v. of _Works_, Dublin, 1775.]
10129{_}1847{_}The great lawyer, Blackstone, says he went from church to church to hear noted London preachers, and it was impossible for him to tell from their discourses whether these luminaries were followers of Confucius, Mahomet, or Christ.
10129{_}1848{_}George III.
10129{_}1849{_}felt compelled to address a letter of expostulation to Archbishop Cornwallis for giving balls and routs at Lambeth Palace on Saturday nights, so that they ran into Sunday morning.
10129{_}1850{_}[2] The Church had given hardly a thought to either the religious or secular education of the masses.
10129{_}1851{_}Gross ignorance pervaded the ranks of the poor all over England.
10129{_}1852{_}Although the English Bible was in the people's hands, it was almost a dead letter.
10129{_}1853{_}[Footnote 2: This letter may be found in _The Life and Times of Lady Huntingdon_.]
10129{_}1854{_}But the voice of awakening had been heard in the land.
10129{_}1855{_}George Whitfield, John Wesley, and a few other brave men, whose hearts were roused by the Spirit of God, went up and down the country proclaiming the glad tidings of the cross, which for so long had been as an idle tale to the English people.
10129{_}1856{_}The wave of religious awakening had touched the highest circles of London society; and when Hannah More received her flattering welcome from fashion, wit, and genius in 1773, the spirit of indifference and neglect had given way in a slight degree to a spirit of inquiry and anxious concern.
10129{_}1857{_}There was, however, no perceptible change as yet in the utter worldliness of the times, or in the low standard of morals.
10129{_}1858{_}It was a perilous thing for a young woman like Hannah More, with her enthusiasm, talents, and general attractiveness, to be suddenly launched in the turbid though fascinating current of London society.
10129{_}1859{_}But the admirable training in strict moral principles with which she had been privileged furnished weapons of defence against the more specious temptations which presented themselves; whilst her quick discernment easily penetrated the thin shell of external polish covering worthlessness of character.
10129{_}1860{_}It was also fortunate for her that at the outset of her London experience she became acquainted with such a sterling man as Dr. Johnson.
10129{_}1861{_}A few days after her arrival she was introduced to David Garrick and his wife.
10129{_}1862{_}The famous actor had seen a letter of hers to a mutual friend, extolling one of his theatrical performances.
10129{_}1863{_}He forthwith secured an interview, which resulted in favourable impressions on both sides, of amiability and intellectual powers.
10129{_}1864{_}A very cordial friendship ensued.
10129{_}1865{_}Garrick's social circle was now thrown open to Miss More.
10129{_}1866{_}At his house she first met Mrs. Elizabeth Montague, the authoress of an _Essay on the Writings and Genius of Shakespeare_, a work which brought around the writer the best literary men of the time.
10129{_}1867{_}Miss More's introduction to Dr. Johnson took place at the house of Sir Joshua Reynolds.
10129{_}1868{_}This event, though much desired, was not without dread, lest the great man should happen to be in one of his querulous moods.
10129{_}1869{_}All fear vanished on her seeing the Doctor approach with a smile on his rugged countenance, and Sir Joshua's macaw perched on his hand.
10129{_}1870{_}Her surprise may be imagined when he greeted her with a verse from a Morning Hymn of her own composition.
10129{_}1871{_}The following extracts are from letters written by one of Hannah's vivacious sisters.
10129{_}1872{_}"Since I last wrote, Hannah has been introduced by Miss Reynolds to Baretti and to Edmund Burke (the 'Sublime and Beautiful' Edmund Burke!).
10129{_}1873{_}From a large party of literary persons assembled at Sir Joshua's she received the most encouraging compliments; and the spirit with which she returned them was acknowledged by all present, as Miss Reynolds informed poor us.
10129{_}1874{_}Miss R. repeats her little poem by heart, with which also the great Johnson is much pleased."
10129{_}1875{_}"We have paid another visit to Miss Reynolds.
10129{_}1876{_}She had sent to engage Dr. Percy (Percy's collection,--now you know him), who is quite a sprightly modern, instead of a rusty antique, as I expected.
10129{_}1877{_}He was no sooner gone, than the most amiable and obliging of women (Miss Reynolds) ordered the coach to take us to Dr. Johnson's _very own house_; yes, Abyssinia's Johnson!
10129{_}1878{_}Dictionary Johnson!
10129{_}1879{_}Rambler's, Idler's, and Irene's Johnson!
10129{_}1880{_}Can you picture to yourself the palpitation of our hearts as we approached his mansion?
10129{_}1881{_}The conversation turned upon a new work of his (the Tour to the Hebrides), and his old friend Richardson ... Miss Reynolds told the doctor of all our rapturous exclamations on the road.
10129{_}1882{_}He shook his scientific head at Hannah, and said, 'She was a _silly thing_.'
10129{_}1883{_}When our visit was ended, he called for his hat, as it rained, to attend us down a very long entry to our coach, and not Rasselas could have acquitted himself more _en cavalier_.
10129{_}1884{_}We are engaged with him at Sir Joshua's, Wednesday evening.
10129{_}1885{_}What do you think of us?"
10129{_}1886{_}A second visit to London took place in the following year, and a third--prolonged to six months--in 1776.
10129{_}1887{_}From this period down to about 1789 Miss More usually spent some time every year amongst her London friends, but chiefly with Mrs. Garrick, either at the Adelphi or at her country residence at Hampton.
10129{_}1888{_}Her "Life," written by Mr. Roberts and others, is rich with letters, which of themselves form a striking autobiography, revealing the writer's prominent phases of character, her steadfast adhesion to high principles, her progress in the path of literary fame, her wearying of fashionable society, and the gradual consecration of all her powers to the service of God.
10129{_}1889{_}Besides these personal matters, we get glimpses of the notable people with whom she was brought into contact, and of the moral and religious condition of the higher classes.
10129{_}1890{_}These letters conform to Hannah More's own idea of what epistolary effusions between friends should be.
10129{_}1891{_}"What I want in a letter," she once wrote, "is the picture of my friend's mind, and the common course of his life.
10129{_}1892{_}I want to know what he is saying and doing; I want him to turn out the inside of his heart to me, without disguise, without appearing better than he is."
10129{_}1893{_}We can therefore obtain a more lifelike portraiture by making extracts from her correspondence than by attempting the task in any other way.
10129{_}1894{_}Describing her feelings in associating with persons of rank and wit, she says:--"I had yesterday the pleasure of dining in Hill Street, Berkeley Square, at a certain Mrs. Montague's, a name not totally obscure.
10129{_}1895{_}The party consisted of herself, Mrs. Carter, Dr. Johnson, Solander, and Matty, Mrs. Boscawen, Miss Reynolds, and Sir Joshua (the idol of every company); some other persons of high rank and less wit, and your humble servant,--a party that would not have disgraced the table of Laelius or of Atticus.
10129{_}1896{_}I felt myself a worm for the consequence which was given me, by mixing me with such a society; but as I told Mrs. Boscawen, and with great truth, I had an opportunity of making an experiment of my heart, by which I learnt that I was not envious, for I certainly did not repine at being the meanest person in company...Dr. Johnson asked me how I liked the new tragedy of Braganza.
10129{_}1897{_}I was afraid to speak before them all, as I knew a diversity of opinion prevailed among the company: however, as I thought it less evil to dissent from the opinion of a fellow-creature than to tell a falsity, I ventured to give my sentiments, and was satisfied with Johnson's answering, 'You are right, madam.'"
10129{_}1898{_}Her conscience was uneasy from visiting the opera, and also from attending Sunday parties, which were greatly in vogue.
10129{_}1899{_}She thus wrote on this subject:--                                             "London, 1775.
10129{_}1900{_}"'Bear me, some god, oh, quickly bear me hence,        To wholesome solitude, the nurse of--'  "'Sense' I was going to add, in the words of Pope, till I recollected that _pence_ had a more appropriate meaning, and was as good a rhyme.
10129{_}1901{_}This apostrophe broke from me on coming from the opera, the first I ever _did_, the last I trust I ever _shall_ go to.
10129{_}1902{_}For what purpose has the Lord of the universe made His creature man with a comprehensive mind?
10129{_}1903{_}Why make him a little lower than the angels?
10129{_}1904{_}Why give him the faculty of thinking, the powers of wit and memory; and, to crown all, an immortal and never-dying spirit?
10129{_}1905{_}Why all this wondrous waste, this prodigality of bounty, if the mere animal senses of sight and hearing (by which he is not distinguished from the brutes that perish) would have answered the end as well?
10129{_}1906{_}and yet I find the same people are seen at the opera every night--an amusement written in a language the greater part of them do not understand, and performed by such a set of beings!...
10129{_}1907{_}Conscience had done its office before; nay was busy at the time; and if it did not dash the cup of pleasure to the ground, infused at least a tincture of wormwood into it.
10129{_}1908{_}I _did_ think of the alarming call, 'What doest thou here, Elijah?'
10129{_}1909{_}and I thought of it to-night at the opera."
10129{_}1910{_}The attractions of wealth and fame had not blinded her to the need of seeking satisfaction from a higher source.
10129{_}1911{_}"For my own part, the more I see of the 'honoured, famed, and great,' the more I see of the littleness, the unsatisfactoriness of all created good; and that no earthly pleasure can fill up the wants of the immortal principle within."
10129{_}1912{_}She was much troubled by the extravagances of fashion in dress and adornments; and, although conforming to some extent to prevailing modes in order to avoid singularity, which she abhorred, she always dressed neatly and decorously, and never, through the whole of her life, wore an article of jewellery simply for ornament.
10129{_}1913{_}The following extract from a letter written by one of Hannah's sisters shows the cordial relationships with Dr. Johnson, and his interest in the five sisters.
10129{_}1914{_}"Tuesday evening we drank tea at Sir Joshua's with Dr. Johnson.
10129{_}1915{_}Hannah is certainly a great favourite.
10129{_}1916{_}She was placed next him, and they had the entire conversation to themselves.
10129{_}1917{_}They were both in remarkably high spirits; it was certainly her lucky night!
10129{_}1918{_}I never heard her say so many good things.
10129{_}1919{_}The old genius was extremely jocular, and the young one very pleasant.
10129{_}1920{_}You would have imagined you had been at some comedy had you heard our peals of laughter.
10129{_}1921{_}They, indeed, tried which could 'pepper the highest,' and it is not clear to me that the lexicographer was really the highest reasoner."
10129{_}1922{_}III.
10129{_}1923{_}CHARACTERISTICS, FRIENDSHIPS, AND EARLY LITERARY WORK.
10129{_}1924{_}Hannah More's flattering reception in London society, and the lively impression which she so quickly created, will give rise to some astonishment in the minds of many readers.
10129{_}1925{_}She had not yet won reputation as an authoress; she did not possess the influence of wealth or of noble family; she was not remarkable for physical beauty; and she had none of the brazen ingenuity of patronage-hunters, by which admission is secured into the houses of distinguished people.
10129{_}1926{_}She came to London a stranger, a plain schoolmistress from Bristol, and yet in a marvellously short time she was one of the best known characters in the ranks of the wise and great.
10129{_}1927{_}The causes of her rapid rise to distinction are not far to seek.
10129{_}1928{_}Her wonderful talent for conversation at once proved an attraction to both men and women.
10129{_}1929{_}But she was not merely a fluent talker, never at a loss for a word, a phrase or a metaphor; had this been her crowning recommendation, Dr. Johnson's long-standing friendship would never have been gained.
10129{_}1930{_}Her talk was always sensible--the outcome of a well-furnished, retentive mind.
10129{_}1931{_}Her judgment was sound, her discrimination delicate, and her grasp of fundamental truths consistently firm.
10129{_}1932{_}She did not accommodate her opinions to meet the exigencies of different coteries, nor was she addicted to compromise.
10129{_}1933{_}She was equally at ease in discussing the merits of _Rasselas_ with Dr. Johnson, the curiosities of art with Lord Orford, Roman history with Gibbon, and the state of the Church with Bishop Porteus.
10129{_}1934{_}Not that she pretended equality of learning with such men, but she had just sufficient knowledge of various subjects to provoke a conversation, and enough cleverness to sustain it by "drawing out" the scholar who might be seated at her side.
10129{_}1935{_}But this was not all.
10129{_}1936{_}Her conversation sparkled with wit and repartee.
10129{_}1937{_}"The mind laughed," says her friend Zachary Macaulay, "not the muscles; the countenance sparkled, but it was with an ethereal flame: everything was oxygen gas and intellectual champagne: and the eye, which her sisters called 'diamond,' and which the painters complained they could not put upon canvas, often gave signal by its coruscation, as the same sort of eye did in her friend Mr. Wilberforce, that something was forthcoming which in a less amiable and religiously disciplined mind might have been very pretty satire, but which glanced off innoxiously in the shape of epigrammatic playfulness."
10129{_}1938{_}[Illustration: ]  Her genial disposition and good temper disarmed difference of opinion of anything harsh or unpleasant, and formed another credential for the prominence she attained in society.
10129{_}1939{_}The absence of all artificiality in sentiment and manners, when contrasted with the straining after effect acquired by fashionably-bred ladies, also added to her attractions in the eyes of thoughtful men.
10129{_}1940{_}But whilst to these causes may be attributed her rapid rise into favour, it was undoubtedly owing to her unswerving and unassuming piety that she retained for so long the respect, confidence, and affection of varied orders of mind in London society.
10129{_}1941{_}At first she appears to have done little to enforce religious teaching amongst her acquaintances.
10129{_}1942{_}Her moral and religious principles were known by the firm stand she took against common incentives to dissipation and irreligion--such as card-playing and Sunday entertainments--against the introduction of questionable topics, unseemly language, and vacuous frivolity into conversation.
10129{_}1943{_}Her religious influence, thus far, was almost a silent or negative one; but it had its effect on others, and laid the foundation of that direct searching and far-reaching influence, which, under the Divine blessing, she wielded in later years.
10129{_}1944{_}Her interest in young people was notably illustrated by her efforts to foster the intellectual tastes of Lord Macaulay when a lad.
10129{_}1945{_}She supplied him with standard books, which formed the nucleus of an excellent library, and advised him in his studies.
10129{_}1946{_}To the child of six she thus writes:--"Though you are a little boy now, you will one day, if it please God, be a man; but long before you are a man I hope you will be a scholar[1]."
10129{_}1947{_}[Footnote 1: See _Life and Letters of Lord Macaulay_, by George Otto Trevelyan, M.P., vol.
10129{_}1948{_}1. pp.
10129{_}1949{_}35, 36]  When Hannah More began to produce books her reputation rose to literary fame.
10129{_}1950{_}In 1775 she wrote a romantic poem, entitled _Sir Eldred of the Bouer_, with which was published another poem, written earlier, _The Bleeding Rock_.
10129{_}1951{_}In the first the element of religion was not forgotten; and both works met with a flattering reception.
10129{_}1952{_}Though, as we have seen, a woman of high Christian tone, with what we should consider strange inconsistency, she both wrote plays, which were acted, and attended the theatre herself.
10129{_}1953{_}In 1777 her tragedy, _Percy_, was brought out at Covent Garden Theatre.
10129{_}1954{_}One of the results of this venture was a shower of invitations to the author of the play from a new circle of titled and distinguished people.
10129{_}1955{_}The play was afterwards translated into German, and performed at Vienna with notable success.
10129{_}1956{_}On the death of Garrick in 1779, Hannah More broke off attendance at the theatre.
10129{_}1957{_}Garrick's widow sought relief and solace in Hannah's company, and for many years a close friendship was kept up between the two ladies, although there could be but little intercourse on religious matters, Mrs. Garrick being a Roman Catholic.
10129{_}1958{_}Before the actor's death Miss More had completed another play, _The Fatal Falsehood_, which was afterwards performed, and which elicited almost as much applause as _Percy_.
10129{_}1959{_}Miss More's experience of fashionable life had now lasted about six years.
10129{_}1960{_}As her fame increased, her taste for society declined.
10129{_}1961{_}The constant round of dinner-parties, conversation-parties, and assemblies of intellect and wealth, though at first full of attraction to one of her disposition, had begun to lose its charm.
10129{_}1962{_}Her depth of character and her recognition of the claims of religion demanded a more satisfactory mode of spending her time and utilising her talents.
10129{_}1963{_}For the next five years we find her often the guest of Mrs. Garrick, but gradually detaching herself from fashionable circles, studying theology, history, and science, writing poems, and engaged in other literary work.
10129{_}1964{_}Her chief literary work during this period consisted of _Sacred Dramas--Moses in the Bulrushes, David and Goliath, Belshazzar_, and _Daniel_.
10129{_}1965{_}She was prompted to this undertaking by a desire to provide, not plays for the stage, but a substitute for some of the pernicious literature of the day which fell into the hands of young people, and also to afford instruction in the common facts of Scripture, The gross ignorance of the Bible amongst fashionable people astonished her one day, when Sir Joshua Reynolds told her that on showing his picture of Samuel to some great patrons they asked him who Samuel was?
10129{_}1966{_}The work answered the purpose for which it was intended, and passed through nineteen editions, receiving high commendation from Bishop Lowth and others.
10129{_}1967{_}Her poem _Sensibility_ was also included in this successful volume.
10129{_}1968{_}A poem, _The Bas Bleu, or Conversation_, written in a lively and facetious strain, owed its origin to the mistakes of a foreigner who gave the literal designation of the _Bas-Bleu_ to a party of friends who had been humorously called the "Blue Stockings."
10129{_}1969{_}At the King's request a manuscript copy of the poem was sent to him; and Dr. Johnson went so far in his praise of the effusion as to say that there was no name in poetry that might not be glad to own it.
10129{_}1970{_}A little later Miss More wrote _Florio_, a poem describing the occupation of a young man of fashion, and his final escape from a life of pleasure to one of usefulness.
10129{_}1971{_}By the death of Dr. Johnson in 1784, Miss More lost the best friend she ever had in London.
10129{_}1972{_}She had been with the Doctor at his last communion at St. Clement's Church, and saw too plainly his altered condition.
10129{_}1973{_}Bound to each other by strong intellectual and stronger religious sympathies, the separation caused a void in Miss More's life which was never afterwards filled.
10129{_}1974{_}Theirs was a friendship born at first sight.
10129{_}1975{_}For more than ten years it grew and flourished, with mutual benefit and happiness to the stern moralist and his promising _protege_.
10129{_}1976{_}Whilst the rugged common-sense and sound literary judgments of the Doctor imparted increasing accuracy and insight to his friend's views of the world and of literature, it was the sparkle, freshness, and wit of Miss More's conversation, and her light-heartedness of character, that often dispelled the clouds of depression from the mental horizon of her sage and trusty adviser, and smoothed the rough edges of his outspoken opinions.
10129{_}1977{_}In religion, it was probably the Doctor's uncompromising fidelity to first principles, and to a fearless practice of truth, that helped to fortify his "dear child," as he called Miss More, in maintaining her integrity amidst the bewildering voices and garish scenes of Vanity Fair.
10129{_}1978{_}IV.
10129{_}1979{_}COWSLIP GREEN.
10129{_}1980{_}About the time of Dr. Johnson's death, in 1784, Hannah More became the possessor of a rural spot, called Cowslip Green, some ten miles from Bristol.
10129{_}1981{_}Here she built herself a cottage, intending to make it her place of retirement for a large portion of each year.
10129{_}1982{_}In the cultivation of her garden she found leisure for reflection as well as an opportunity to pursue a favourite occupation.
10129{_}1983{_}The inroads which death had made in her circle of intimate friends, a growing dissatisfaction with the enjoyments of London life, and especially a keener sense of her responsibility, as a professed Christian, than she had hitherto experienced, led to a close self-examination, and to a scrutiny of the real motives of her life.
10129{_}1984{_}The result of this testing process showed itself in various ways.
10129{_}1985{_}During occasional visits to London and attendance at parties she lost no opportunity of enforcing the truths of religion.
10129{_}1986{_}Her silent witnessing was now exchanged for active exertion.
10129{_}1987{_}The manners and practices of people who were amongst her most effusive admirers sometimes met with her indignant rebuke.
10129{_}1988{_}Ladies of title, society beauties, and leaders of fashion, who were unapproachable by other religious influences, she urged in private to consider their spiritual interests.
10129{_}1989{_}The method she adopted was not, usually, to start religious topics, but "to extract from common subjects some useful and awful truth, and to counteract the mischief of a popular sentiment by one drawn from religion."
10129{_}1990{_}Perhaps a message which John Wesley once sent to her through a sister may have weighed considerably in deterring her from an entire severance from the fashionable world.
10129{_}1991{_}"Tell her to live in the world; _there_ is the sphere of her usefulness; they will not let _us_ come nigh them."
10129{_}1992{_}Not content with personal and private reproof, advice, and entreaty, she now devoted her pen to the denunciation of folly and vice in high places.
10129{_}1993{_}In her work, _Thoughts on the Importance of the Manners of the Great to General Society_, whilst protesting against prevalent irreligious practices and habits of dissipation, which even good people sanctioned, she sought to arouse a sensitive regard for mutual responsibility as set forth in the New Testament.
10129{_}1994{_}In 1788 the slave trade formed a burning question in Parliament.
10129{_}1995{_}Miss More, intensely aroused by the descriptions presented of the horrible traffic, found vent for her feelings in a poem on the subject.
10129{_}1996{_}About the same time a close friendship began with Wilberforce, which lasted to the end of life.
10129{_}1997{_}A yet more important friendship commenced at this period--one that was destined to work a powerful influence on Miss More's life.
10129{_}1998{_}The Rev.
10129{_}1999{_}John Newton, one of the leaders amongst the evangelical clergy, held the incumbency of St. Mary Woolnoth.
10129{_}2000{_}Attendance on his ministry led to a correspondence and a deep friendship.
10129{_}2001{_}John Newton was precisely the kind of man whom Hannah More needed to assist her in spiritual progress, and to direct her steps into paths of settled peace.
10129{_}2002{_}Her letters to Mr. Newton, stating her difficulties and seeking counsel, breathe the spirit of the humble and sincere scholar of Christ.
10129{_}2003{_}Her willingness to obey the Master whom she professed to serve, and her earnest desire to be brought into closer relations with God, although checked, had never been stifled by the claims of intellect or by the attractions of the world.
10129{_}2004{_}From this time the work of the Holy Spirit in deepening her love for the Saviour became more and more prominent.
10129{_}2005{_}Turning for a time from Christian work amongst the rich, Miss More now devoted her efforts to the improvement of the moral and religious condition of the poor.
10129{_}2006{_}About ten miles from Cowslip Green was the picturesque village of Cheddar, the population of which was sunk in ignorance and depravity.
10129{_}2007{_}The incumbent lived at Oxford, and the curate at Wells, twelve miles off.
10129{_}2008{_}There was but one service a week, and no pastoral visitation whatever.
10129{_}2009{_}There were thirteen parishes in the neighbourhood without even a resident curate.
10129{_}2010{_}Drunkenness and utter inefficiency prevailed to a terrible extent amongst the clergy in this district; whilst education was a question that never troubled either the clergy or the people.
10129{_}2011{_}At Cheddar Hannah and her sister Patty opened a school; and in a short time nearly 300 children attended regularly.
10129{_}2012{_}The sisters had to combat strong prejudices amongst the farmers.
10129{_}2013{_}By dint of much persuasion and flattery the opposing forces were at length won over, even to hearty concurrence.
10129{_}2014{_}Masters and mistresses were procured for teaching reading, seeing, knitting, and spinning, and giving religious instruction on Sundays.
10129{_}2015{_}A second school was shortly opened in an adjoining parish, the vicarage-house, which had remained uninhabited for a hundred years, having been put into repair for the purpose.
10129{_}2016{_}During 1790 Miss More published a volume entitled, _An Estimate of the Religion of the Fashionable World_.
10129{_}2017{_}The book was quickly bought up, and within two years reached a fifth edition.
10129{_}2018{_}The prevailing indifference to vital religion, the corruptions of society, the decline of domestic piety, and the absence of religion from the education of the upper classes were the themes treated by the writer with unsparing candour and convincing force.
10129{_}2019{_}Encouraged by her success at Cheddar, Miss More, with her sister Patty, went further afield, and selected two mining villages on the top of the Mendip Hills as the next scene of her labours.
10129{_}2020{_}The difficulties here were even greater than those at Cheddar.
10129{_}2021{_}The neighbourhood was so bad, we are told, that no constable would venture to execute his office there.
10129{_}2022{_}Friends warned the Misses More that their lives would be in danger if they persisted in their project.
10129{_}2023{_}The people imagined that the sisters had come to make money by kidnapping their children for slaves.
10129{_}2024{_}Undaunted by obstacles and perils, the workers persevered, until in no less than ten parishes schools were commenced, which, before long, were attended by 1200 children.
10129{_}2025{_}In every parish the acquiescence of the incumbent was first obtained before proceeding to open a school.
10129{_}2026{_}At the evening meetings, to which adults were invited, a simple sermon was read by one of the sisters, and also a printed prayer and a psalm.
10129{_}2027{_}Few mistresses could be found who had not owed their religious impressions to Wesleyan influence; and thus Hannah More was subsequently, though mistakenly, thought to be a Methodist.
10129{_}2028{_}Although influenced by the Methodist revival, she always considered and professed herself to be a member of the Episcopal Church.
10129{_}2029{_}Whilst immersed in her village work, she was earnestly solicited to write a popular tract that might help to counteract the baneful influence of Jacobin and infidel publications, and infamous ballads, which were now scattered broadcast over England.
10129{_}2030{_}She declined the task, doubtful of her efficiency to produce a pamphlet equal to the occasion.
10129{_}2031{_}On second thoughts, however, she tried her powers in secret, and issued anonymously a lively dialogue called _Village Politics_, by "Will Chip."
10129{_}2032{_}The success was phenomenal.
10129{_}2033{_}Friends ignorant of the authorship sent her copies by every post within three or four days of publication, begging her to distribute the pamphlet as widely as possible.
10129{_}2034{_}In a short time copies were to be found in all parts of the kingdom.
10129{_}2035{_}Hundreds of thousands were circulated in London.
10129{_}2036{_}Such was the enthusiasm that private persons printed large editions at their own expense, whilst the Government sent off quantities to Scotland and Ireland.
10129{_}2037{_}At last the secret came out; and the author was deluged with congratulations and thanks.
10129{_}2038{_}Some persons of sound judgment declared that _Village Politic_ had essentially contributed, under Providence, to prevent a revolution, whilst others went so far as to allege that Miss More had "wielded at will the fierce democratie of England, and stemmed the tide of misguided opinion."
10129{_}2039{_}A little later Miss More wrote another pamphlet, by way of reply to the atheistical speech of Dupont to the National Convention, and devoted the profits, amounting to L240, towards the relief of the French emigrant clergy.
10129{_}2040{_}In 1794, or early in 1795, she commenced the issue of tracts.
10129{_}2041{_}This was a form of literary work not much used in those days.
10129{_}2042{_}The founders of the Religious Tract Society, realising the value of this kind of work, but considering that Miss More's tracts needed supplementing with some which should in every case contain the simple communication of the Gospel, began in 1799 to undertake the dissemination of religious knowledge.
10129{_}2043{_}Sunday schools, through the energy of Mr. Raikes, were rising in various parts of the country; the poorer classes were learning to read; and nothing in the shape of cheap literature was provided to meet their new craving, except mischievous broadsheets and worthless doggerel.
10129{_}2044{_}Hannah More set to work to supply something healthy to amuse, instruct, and edify the new order of readers.
10129{_}2045{_}She produced regularly every month for three years, three tracts--simple, pithy, vivacious, consisting of stories, ballads, homilies, and prayers.
10129{_}2046{_}She was sometimes assisted by one of her sisters and two or three friends; but the burden of the work, including heavy correspondence with local committees in almost every district of England, fell upon her shoulders.
10129{_}2047{_}In order to issue the brochures at a cheap rate and to undersell pernicious publications, she found it necessary to raise a subscription.
10129{_}2048{_}Her appeal met with a liberal response; and very shortly the lively tracts, with a rough woodcut on the title-page, came by thousands from the printer's hands.
10129{_}2049{_}In the first year no less than two millions were sold.
10129{_}2050{_}Amongst the tracts were _The Shepherd of Salisbury Plain, Black Giles the Poacher, History of Mr. Fantom, The Two Shoemakers, History of Tom White the Postilion, The Strait Gate and the Broad Way;_ and amongst the ballads _Turning the Carpet, King Dionysius and Squire Damocles, The Honest Miller of Gloucestershire, The Gin-Shop, or A Peep into a Prison_.
10129{_}2051{_}It would be difficult to over-estimate both the direct and secondary value of the Cheap Repository Tracts.
10129{_}2052{_}Their beneficial influence must have been incalculable; and for this reason they should be placed amongst the greatest and best work of Hannah More's useful life.
10129{_}2053{_}By 1798 Miss More had withdrawn almost entirely from London society, contenting herself with a yearly visit of two months, which she divided between Mrs. Garrick, Bishop Porteus, Lord Teignmouth, and one or two others.
10129{_}2054{_}Her schools occupied the best part of her time; but frequent attacks of illness often interfered with her duties.
10129{_}2055{_}In 1799 her active pen was at work again.
10129{_}2056{_}Her third ethical publication, _Strictures on Female Education_, came out, forming yet another counterblast to the corrupt systems in vogue amongst the wealthy classes.
10129{_}2057{_}It would have been marvellous had Miss More escaped persecution in her work amongst rural populations.
10129{_}2058{_}Combating prejudices, introducing unheard-of innovations, adopting plans which rumour stated were deeply tainted with Methodism (and therefore bad, according to clerical and general opinion in those days), she had to encounter at last a pitiless storm of hostility.
10129{_}2059{_}This violent and prolonged attack, whilst it showed to what infamous lengths the tongues of slander, envy, and bigotry could go in attempting to destroy a noble woman's reputation, tested to the utmost Hannah More's fine qualities of Christian forbearance and courage.
10129{_}2060{_}V.  BARLEY WOOD, CLOSING YEAES AND DEATH.
10129{_}2061{_}In 1802 Miss More removed from Cowslip Green to a house which she had built at Barley Wood, about a mile distant.
10129{_}2062{_}Soon afterwards her sisters, having disposed of their house at Bath, came to live with her.
10129{_}2063{_}For the next twenty years, or more, friends from all parts sought her society, and strangers of all ages and of all ranks came for advice, sympathy, and help.
10129{_}2064{_}Her immense correspondence occupied a very large portion of her time.
10129{_}2065{_}There was scarcely a person at all prominent in the religious world who was not brought into association with her.
10129{_}2066{_}Miss More's prolonged life did not close until 1833, when she had arrived at her eighty-ninth year.
10129{_}2067{_}The thirty-one years that remained to her after quitting Cowslip Green was as full of work and usefulness as the previous part of her life.
10129{_}2068{_}It will be impossible within the space now left to do more than indicate the chief events of this period, which was not remarkable for any fresh departure either in educational or religious work.
10129{_}2069{_}Miss More had already marked out for herself two distinct and definite lines of usefulness--the education of the poor, and the improvement of morals and religion amongst the rich.
10129{_}2070{_}By her active exertion and by her busy pen she continued to pursue these two lines of work down to the year of her death.
10129{_}2071{_}It must be remembered that she was a martyr during these latter years to long attacks of illness, one of which almost completely prostrated her for two years; and when upwards of seventy she was unable to leave the house for more than seven years.
10129{_}2072{_}At this period she stated that she had never been free from pain for long together since she was ten years old.
10129{_}2073{_}Such physical hindrances render her persistent activity and the great work she accomplished all the more remarkable.
10129{_}2074{_}When not entirely incapacitated she still worked with her pen, attended to business connected with her schools, and received visitors in the sick room.
10129{_}2075{_}It used to be said amongst her friends that when she was laid aside they always expected a new book from her.
10129{_}2076{_}In 1805 she published _Hints towards forming the Character of a Young Princess_.
10129{_}2077{_}It was undertaken, at the request of a bishop, with reference to the education of the Princess Charlotte.
10129{_}2078{_}In 1809 her religious novel, _Coelebs in Search of a Wife_, issued anonymously, roused universal attention.
10129{_}2079{_}In twelve months as many editions came out; and during the author's lifetime thirty editions of a thousand copies each were printed in America.
10129{_}2080{_}This was followed shortly by _Practical Piety_, which soon ran to the tenth edition, and which brought the author to the end of her life numerous gratifying testimonies of its results.
10129{_}2081{_}As a sequel to this work, _Christian Morals_ was published in 1812, and was also widely circulated.
10129{_}2082{_}Three years later, when the author had entered her seventieth year, she wrote an _Essay on the Character and Writings of St. Paul_, in two volumes, which, notwithstanding absorbing political events, was received with the same eagerness which greeted her former works.
10129{_}2083{_}_Moral Sketches of Prevailing Opinions and Manners, Foreign and Domestic_, was published in 1819, being chiefly directed against the rage for copying French customs and manners.
10129{_}2084{_}At the age of eighty-two she collected from her later works her _Thoughts on Prayer_ and re-issued them in a little volume, with a short preface.
10129{_}2085{_}This was her last literary effort.
10129{_}2086{_}She said to a friend that the only remarkable thing which belonged to her as an author was that she had written eleven volumes after the age of sixty.
10129{_}2087{_}Between 1813 and 1818 her four sisters died.
10129{_}2088{_}The last to go was Martha, Hannah's trusty helpmeet and lieutenant in all her benevolent schemes, and her tender consoler in many a season of sickness.
10129{_}2089{_}Soon after this event Miss More's long illness of seven years occurred.
10129{_}2090{_}Unable to give proper supervision to her servants, she was victimised in household matters in various ways.
10129{_}2091{_}Extravagance and misconduct at length gave rise to scandal; and at the representation of friends Miss More reluctantly decided to break up her establishment, and remove to another and smaller residence at Clifton.
10129{_}2092{_}It was with a sad heart that she left her charming dwelling; and as she glanced back into the beautiful garden, with its shady bowers, she exclaimed, "I am driven, like Eve, out of Paradise; but not, like Eve, by angels."
10129{_}2093{_}She lived five and a half years at Clifton, tranquilly waiting for the end, and attending, as far as failing strength would permit, to the distribution of her charities, the work of her schools and the entertainment of friends.
10129{_}2094{_}Almost to the last she retained unimpaired the use of her faculties.
10129{_}2095{_}The intellectual vivacity of early days often reappeared.
10129{_}2096{_}During one of her illnesses some one remarked, in allusion to the struggle of the remnant of sin in a person recently awakened to the truth, "The old man dies hard!"
10129{_}2097{_}"The old woman dies hard!"
10129{_}2098{_}exclaimed the invalid.
10129{_}2099{_}At eighty-three she said, "I have too many petty cares at that age when the grasshopper is a burden.
10129{_}2100{_}I have _many_ grasshoppers, and seem to have less time and more labour than ever."
10129{_}2101{_}Her last days were spent almost entirely in prayer, invoking blessings on those around her and on the village work which lay so near her heart.
10129{_}2102{_}She said to a friend during her last illness, "To go to heaven, think what _that_ is!
10129{_}2103{_}to go to my Saviour who died that I might live!
10129{_}2104{_}Lord, humble me, subdue every evil temper in me.
10129{_}2105{_}May we meet in a robe of glory!
10129{_}2106{_}Through Christ's merits alone can we be saved... Lord, I believe--I _do_ believe with all the powers of my weak, sinful heart.
10129{_}2107{_}Lord Jesus, look down upon me from Thy holy habitation; strengthen my faith, and quicken me in my preparation.
10129{_}2108{_}Support me in that trying hour when I most need it!
10129{_}2109{_}It is a glorious thing to die!"
10129{_}2110{_}No vanity or self-praise on the ground of her life's labours ever found a place in her thoughts.
10129{_}2111{_}Some one began to speak of her good deeds.
10129{_}2112{_}"Talk not so vainly," she exclaimed; "I utterly cast them from me, and fall low at the foot of the cross."
10129{_}2113{_}She sank gradually, and without pain, and on September 7, 1833, quietly passed away.
10129{_}2114{_}There are few thoughtful students who will hesitate to rank Hannah More with the leading religious and educational reformers of the eighteenth century.
10129{_}2115{_}In essential matters she was a kindred spirit with Whitfield, Wesley, Raikes, and others, and worked, in the way marked out for her by God, for the regeneration of her country.
10129{_}2116{_}With regard to her books, she believed they would be little read after her death.
10129{_}2117{_}To a considerable extent her judgment has been verified.
10129{_}2118{_}Her writings were a continual seed-sowing, which later workers fertilised, and brought to maturity.
10129{_}2119{_}They were republished in eleven volumes in 1830.
10129{_}2120{_}Besides the prominence given to their religious or moral purpose, most of them are remarkable for sustained fervour, persuasiveness of tone, and practical common sense.
10129{_}2121{_}We give a few extracts from some of the principal works, to illustrate Hannah More's methods of appealing to the conscience and awakening spiritual concern.
10129{_}2122{_}"There are two things of which a wise man will be scrupulously careful--his conscience and his credit.
10129{_}2123{_}Happily, they are almost inseparable concomitants; they are commonly kept or lost together; the same things which wound the one usually giving a blow to the other; yet it must be confessed, that conscience and a mere worldly credit are not, in all instances, allowed to subsist together....  "Between a wounded conscience and a wounded credit, there is the same difference as between a crime and a calamity.
10129{_}2124{_}Of two inevitable evils, religion instructs us to submit to that which is inferior and involuntary.
10129{_}2125{_}As much as reputation exceeds every worldly good, so much, and far more, is conscience to be consulted before credit--if credit that can be called, which is derived from the acclamations of a mob, whether composed of 'the great vulgar or the small'"--_Christian Morals_ (chapter xxiv.).
10129{_}2126{_}"One cause, therefore, of the dulness of many Christians in prayer, is their slight acquaintance with the sacred volume.
10129{_}2127{_}They hear it periodically, they read it occasionally, they are contented to know it historically, to consider it superficially; but they do not endeavour to get their minds imbued with its spirit.
10129{_}2128{_}If they store their memory with its facts, they do not impress their hearts with its truths.
10129{_}2129{_}They do not regard it as the nutriment on which their spiritual life and growth depend.
10129{_}2130{_}They do not pray over it; they do not consider all its doctrines as of practical application; they do not cultivate that spiritual discernment which alone can enable them judiciously to appropriate its promises, and apply its denunciations to their own actual case.
10129{_}2131{_}They do not use it as an unerring line, to ascertain their own rectitude, or detect their own obliquities."
10129{_}2132{_}*       *       *       *       *  "The discrepancies between our prayers and our practice do not end here.
10129{_}2133{_}How frequently are we solemnly imploring of God that 'His kingdom may come,' while we are doing nothing to promote His kingdom of grace here, and consequently His kingdom of glory hereafter."
10129{_}2134{_}*       *       *       *       *  "Prayer draws all the Christian graces into its focus.
10129{_}2135{_}It draws Charity, followed by her lovely train, her forbearance with faults, her forgiveness of injuries, her pity for errors, her compassion for want.
10129{_}2136{_}It draws Repentance, with her holy sorrows, her pious resolutions, her self-distrust.
10129{_}2137{_}It attracts Faith, with her elevated eye,--Hope, with her grasped anchor,--Beneficence, with her open hand,--Zeal, looking far and wide to serve,--Humility, with introverted eye, looking at home.
10129{_}2138{_}Prayer, by quickening these graces in the heart warms them into life, fits them for service, and dismisses each to it appropriate practice.
10129{_}2139{_}Cordial prayer is mental virtue; Christian virtue is spiritual action."
10129{_}2140{_}--_The Spirit of Prayer_ (chapters iii., viii., and xi.).
10129{_}2141{_}"If good we plant not, vice will fill the place,       And rankest weeds the richest soils deface.
10129{_}2142{_}Learn how ungoverned thoughts the mind pervert,       And to disease all nourishment convert.
10129{_}2143{_}Ah!
10129{_}2144{_}happy she, whose wisdom learns to find       A healthful fancy, and a well-trained mind.
10129{_}2145{_}A sick man's wildest dreams less wild are found       Than the day-visions of a mind unsound.
10129{_}2146{_}Disordered phantasies indulged too much.
10129{_}2147{_}Like harpies, always taint whate'er they touch.
10129{_}2148{_}Fly soothing Solitude!
10129{_}2149{_}fly vain Desire!
10129{_}2150{_}Fly such soft verse as fans the dang'rous fire!
10129{_}2151{_}Seek action; 'tis the scene which virtue loves;       The vig'rous sun not only shines, but moves.
10129{_}2152{_}From sickly thoughts with quick abhorrence start,       And rule the fancy if you'd rule the heart:       By active goodness, by laborious schemes,       Subdue wild visions and delusive dreams.
10129{_}2153{_}No earthly good a Christian's views should bound,       For ever rising should his aims be found.
10129{_}2154{_}Leave that fictitious good your fancy feigns,       For scenes where real bliss eternal reigns:       Look to that region of immortal joys,       Where fear disturbs not, nor possession cloys;       Beyond what Fancy forms of rosy bowers,       Or blooming chaplets of unfading flowers;       Fairer than o'er imagination drew,       Or poet's warmest visions ever knew.
10129{_}2155{_}Press eager onward to these blissful plains,       Where life eternal, joy perpetual reigns."
10129{_}2156{_}_The Search after Happiness_.
10129{_}2157{_}HENRY JOHNSON.
10129{_}2158{_}SUSANNA WESLEY.
10129{_}2159{_}I.  PARENTAGE AND EDUCATION.
10129{_}2160{_}The mother of John Wesley was the daughter of Dr. Samuel Annesley, an eminent minister of the Church of England at the period of the great Civil War.
10129{_}2161{_}He resigned his charge, being one of the two thousand who, after the Restoration, declared for Nonconformity, and preached their farewell sermons in the Established Church, on the 17th of August, 1662.
10129{_}2162{_}He found his sphere in the meeting-house of Little St. Helen's, Bishopsgate.
10129{_}2163{_}Dr. Annesley's second wife, the mother of Susanna, was a woman of eminent piety, and beloved of all who knew her.
10129{_}2164{_}"How many children has Dr.
10129{_}2165{_}Annseley?"
10129{_}2166{_}was a question asked of the eminent Puritan preacher Manton, who had just been officiating at the baptism of one of the number.
10129{_}2167{_}"I believe it is two dozen, or a quarter of a hundred," he replied.
10129{_}2168{_}Such was the family into which the mother of the Wesleys was born on the 20th of January, 1669.
10129{_}2169{_}Of this crowded household, the majority were daughters, and Susanna was the youngest of these.
10129{_}2170{_}In her own Journals, which form the only account of her childhood, we read of several instances of her "preservation from accidents," and once from a "violent death."
10129{_}2171{_}The method of her education is not clearly stated, but "the tree is known by its fruits."
10129{_}2172{_}There is evidence that it was sound and liberal, and up to the best standard of the day in any rank of society.
10129{_}2173{_}French and music were evidently among her attainments, while in her letters and treatises there are abundant tokens that logic and philosophy were also held in effective possession and use.
10129{_}2174{_}She tells us that which might have been expected when she says that she "was early initiated and instructed in the first principles of the Christian religion;" and in after days we find her giving to her son a rule which had proved to be a blessing to her own girlhood--"Never to spend more time in any matter of mere recreation in one day, than I spend in private religious duties."
10129{_}2175{_}The thoroughness of her own "private religious duties" is shown by the fact that in the year 1700 she made a resolution to spend one hour morning and evening in private devotion.
10129{_}2176{_}This practice she kept up through life as far as circumstances would admit.
10129{_}2177{_}II.
10129{_}2178{_}THEOLOGICAL STUDIES.
10129{_}2179{_}Soon we find Susanna Wesley studying the works of Jeremy Taylor, of the early Puritan Divines, and the immortal Bunyan, till at length her vigour of intellect and enterprise in reading led her into danger.
10129{_}2180{_}By reading Arian and Socinian authors of the period, her faith was shaken.
10129{_}2181{_}This, however, was not to be for long, and the manner of her recall was marked by interesting circumstances.
10129{_}2182{_}It is at this juncture that Samuel Wesley, her future husband, first appears in the story as the friend of her soul.
10129{_}2183{_}This young student, seven years her senior, had himself made "proof" of Socinianism.
10129{_}2184{_}In the course of some literary work, he had been specially well paid for the translation of Socinian writings from the Latin; but his strong mind revolted from their principles, the task was resigned, and his faith became more firmly rooted in Christ as the eternal Son of God.
10129{_}2185{_}In this frame of mind Mr. Wesley met Susanna Annesley, and by God's help, succeeded in accomplishing her complete extrication from the meshes of doctrinal error and distress.
10129{_}2186{_}It can be gathered from her writings, about this time, that the salutary change proceeded not out of complaisance to the lover, but by reception of a fulness of light from heaven.
10129{_}2187{_}Clearness, zeal, and love mark her _Meditations and Disquisitions on the Holy Trinity; the Godhead and Atonement of the Lord Jesus Christ; the Personality and Work of the Holy Spirit_.
10129{_}2188{_}Another epoch in the girlhood of this remarkable young lady was the engagement, somewhat previously, of her mind in the controversy between the Church and Nonconformity.
10129{_}2189{_}Here she had ample opportunity of being well-informed, for her father's house was the resort of many able men on both sides of the question.
10129{_}2190{_}The result was that, with all due respect toward her beloved parent, she, renounced his ecclesiastical views and attached herself to the Established Church.
10129{_}2191{_}"I was educated among the Dissenters," she writes, "and because there was something remarkable in my leaving them at so early an age, not being full thirteen, I had drawn up an account of the whole transaction, under which I had included the main of the controversy between them and the Established Church as far as it had come to my knowledge."
10129{_}2192{_}Clearly, Susanna Wesley is not to be considered as having merely accepted the ecclesiastical situation, turning "Churchwoman" by marriage.
10129{_}2193{_}III.
10129{_}2194{_}MARRIAGE.
10129{_}2195{_}Dr. Annesley's daughters were remarkable for their personal beauty, and from all accounts it would seem that the subject of this narrative shared this "dower."
10129{_}2196{_}She was of average stature and slight frame.
10129{_}2197{_}"Some time, late in 1689 or early in 1690," Susanna Annesley was married to Samuel Wesley.
10129{_}2198{_}Mr. Wesley was at that time a curate at a salary of L30 a year, and with his newly-wedded wife, took lodgings in London till the autumn of 1690, when he received the living of South Ormsby, in Lincolnshire, through the presentation of the Marquis of Normanby.
10129{_}2199{_}While exercising, in his pastoral duties, a diligence and faithfulness such as to put him for the most part above censure, the young husband toiled hard in literary work for the support of his household, and by various publications of a theological character in verse and prose--at one time a metrical _Life of Christ_, at another a treatise on _The Hebrew Points_, and chiefly by articles in Dunton's _Athenian Oracle_--he earned the means of keeping his family at least above distress.
10129{_}2200{_}About the close of 1696 Samuel Wesley was presented to the parish of Epworth--a place destined to be irrevocably associated with his name.
